I admit that I hated it at first, but if any song by them has come to grow on me, it's this one. Now I think it's one of their great ballads, even if it's no \\"Wild Horses.\\" Some may find it to be a cheesy love song (therefore the Stones out of their element), but I think it doesn't turn into a joke. It's not amazing, but it's a nice effort, especially the whispered portion. Then we have \\"Silver Train\\", which although is not one of their best rockers, is still enjoyable. The lyrics are funny too if not taken seriously, showing the \\"shoot first and ask questions later\\" philosophy of their one night stands. And I don't think the filler really starts until \\"Hide Your Love\\" which is tedious and \\"Can You Hear the Music\\", which is just too cornball. Then we have the finale, \\"Star Star,\\" the one other great rocker of the album. If you consider yourself to be \\"mild-mannered\\" or \\"easily offended,\\" stay the hell away from this track, though I honestly think any true low down, dirty Stones fan will be right there shouting out that foul, but catchy chorus with Mick. I know I do. So, there you have it (sorry about the lengthiness). Some hate \\"Goats Head Soup\\" for it's quirks, but what I can say, they are fine by me and make things interesting. Check it out, owning one more Stones album won't hurt.

In a Civil Rights demonstration in Northern Ireland, British troops opened fire on protesters when things were getting hairy, which would eventually lead to 27 wounded and 13 dead.  This was a tragedy that struck a major blow to the Civil Rights movement, and to Ireland and Britain as well.<br />From what I understand, this is still a very controversial topic, even today.  Nobody is still 100% sure of what exactly happened.  Both sides are still debating and offering their versions of what really went down.  I don't know much about the event, as I said in the beginning of this review. The movie isn't there to tell a single story or show us \\"characters;\\" the only goal is to try to educate us all on what happened on January 30th. 0312263120|5.0|I was introduced to the books of author Dan Brown only three weeks ago, but have quickly absorbed all four of his published works.  It is easy to see why some are comparing the work of Dan Brown and James BeauSeigneur (THE CHRIST CLONE TRILOGY).  Both Brown and BeauSeigneur deal masterfully with the more mysterious features of religion, politics, and science.  Both bring to light amazing bits of information, which they weave into the intricate patterns of their stories.  Both are highly imaginative and write with a ring of authenticity that makes for a compelling read.  While Brown compresses labyrinthine plots into brief time periods to provide page-turning suspense, BeauSeigneur trilogy is of epic proportion, covering several decades.  While Brown applies the mysteries of history to the drama of \\"today,\\" BeauSeigneur uses both history and prophecy (from perhaps a dozen major world religions) to transport the reader from the world of today, to the very dawning of a new age in a story reminiscent of the scope of Asimov's classic, FOUNDATION. <br />One other difference is that BeauSeigneur has taken the novel (pun intended and forgiveness is asked) approach of including footnotes in his books of fiction.  By doing so, he all but eliminates the necessity of suspending disbelief.  Few authors employ such strong factual grounding as to make footnotes useful, but I believe Brown's work (and his readers) would benefit from BeauSeigneur's innovation.

B000007VCM|4.0|The Cult are quite the enigmatic band, aren't they? Despite roots steeped in Goth as the amalgam of Ian Astbury's old band Southern Death Cult and Billy Duffy's former band Theater of Hate, the Cult's post-DREAMTIME sound seemed to call on a musical influence that even pre-dated Goth. Dare I say it, Metal? No, not the cheesy hair metal of the 80's or death metal or speed metal, but instead the the classic prototypical metal of Black Sabbath, Deep Purple, Uriah Heap, and of course Led Zeppelin. The path towards this musical direction starts with LOVE. Despite the swirling, heavily echoed & flanged guitar work of Billy Duffy, the trippy lyrics of Ian Astbury, song titles like \\"Nirvana\\", and the paisley-tinged artwork, LOVE is still essentially a heavy rock album. The latest remaster is much more bottom heavy, punchier, and of course LOUDER than the earlier versions. It thus finally comes across as the heavy rock masterpiece that it is. Billy's Gretsch White Falcon through Roland JC-120's of the era sounds more like Billy's Les Paul Custom through Marshall stacks of the Cult's latter era. The original Beggar's Banquet LP and CD masters were a bit thin-sounding, and the Sire-released US version was thinner still. As such, one must have gone to a Cult show live back in 1985 to see that they had much more in common musically with AC/DC than with their compadres Love & Rockets or the Bolshoi. <br />With no shortage of power-chords, among this classic album's highlights are the radio & club faves \\"She Sells Sanctuary\\" and \\"Rain\\", and there are treasures like \\"Big Neon Glitter\\" and \\"Phoenix\\". You can tell that the band was just itchin' to totally rock out, but they had to subdue themselves. Why? Was it because of producer Steve Brown, who once produced Wham? Is it mere coincidence that they were finally allowed to rock out to their collective hearts' content on the post-Manor Sessions Rick Rubin -produced ELECTRIC? I think not. I am sure that most die-hard Cult fans, myselfincluded, would have loved for Beggar's Banquet to have included a bonus cut or two from a pool of selections such as \\"Little Face\\", \\"No. 13\\", \\"Assault on Sanctuary\\", All Souls Avenue\\", and \\"Judith\\" just to galvanize these rarities from this era with the album on which they would have appeared if they had made the final cut.<br />LOVE is the first step taken toward the \\"Sonic Temple\\", forward or backward depending on how you look at it. Whether you consider it Goth, neo-psychedelic, or whatever, there's no disputing that LOVE is pure power-chord magic!

B00005JKJA|5.0|I won't bother to add to the already monolithic body of glowing reviews of this film; I think it is a masterful work, equivalent to and perhaps surpassing &quot;Blue Velvet&quot; in artistic merit. I am writing mostly because many of those who claim that they hated the film because it &quot;doesn't make sense,&quot; or loved it even though it is &quot;open to interpretation&quot; may not have taken heed of the clues David Lynch included in the DVD sleeve. They clearly reveal the logic of the film to those who take the requisite time to think them through. My review is essentially one giant &quot;spoiler,&quot; so if you haven't seen the film, take heed.      The film most certainly does &quot;make sense&quot; and follows a completely rational and logistically valid plot structure. The film begins with a stylized jitterbug contest behind the opening credits, showing Naomi Watt's character (Diane Selwyn) winning a trip to LA from her native Canada to tryout for a Hollywood production. We then see the suggestion of a sleeping figure (Diane again) in red sheets prior to the start of her dream, which opens with the hypnotic figure of a limosine traveling down a dark road, containing Diane's idealization of her real-life paramour, Camilla Rhodes. In reality, Camilla is Diane's former lesbian lover, who betrayed her by stealing the coveted role in the film Diane unsuccessfully tried out for, and spurned her affections for the director of the film. Diane is so jealous and infuriated that she hires a hitman to kill Camilla; when the two meet to discuss the deal, the hitman says he will leave a blue key on her coffee table to signify that Camilla has been successfully dispatched. The film's dream sequence begins after Diane has received the key, and Diane's fantasies of a happier outcome are manifest in what we see.       In her dream, she is her idealized self, free of insecurities, more innocent and charismatic--nailing her tryout for the film, but explaining &quot;Camilla's&quot; victory by the influence of the mafia (&quot;Camilla&quot; in the dream is replaced by a woman whom the real-life Camilla tauntingly kisses at a party to infuriate Diane). Other characters who represent real-life counterparts also resurface in the dream, in various roles: &quot;Coco,&quot; played by Ann Miller, is actually the film director's mother, the man terrified of the ghoul behind Winkie's is an accomplice of Diane's hired hitman, and the mafiosos played by Dan Hedaya and Angelo Badalamenti were other attendees of the humiliating party where Camilla taunts Diane with news of her engagement to the director. In the dream, Diane refashions her hitman as a bungling idiot who botches Camilla's murder, subsequently leaving Camilla helpless with amnesia for who she is or where she came from so that &quot;Betty,&quot; Diane's counterpart in the dream, can become her heroine, and have a utopian, romantic love affair with her.       Throughout the dream, omens occur that suggest the truth behind Diane's fantasy; the forboding man behind Winkie's, Lee Grant's wacko Cassandra-character with her warnings of trouble, the Cowboy, and the MC at the late-night Cabaret who insists that all is not as it seems. The blue key becomes expressionistically rendered in the dream, and opens the proverbial Pandora's Box, at which time Diane mysteriously disappears from her own dream, leaving Camilla alone to open the box--and then Lynch imposes a couple of his haunting frame shifts, here done with lighting effects, before the Cowboy enters Diane's bedroom, telling her &quot;it's time to wake up, pretty girl.&quot;      Now we see Diane's reality when she awakens, and evidence of her crushing guilt (notice her initial relief when she hallucinates that Camilla has returned from the dead, and her subsequent breakdown when she realizes the truth). Eventually, the gravity of what she has done overwhelms her when she realizes that the police want her for questioning, and the old couple from her dream, whom I presume represent her conscience, are released by the demon behind Winkie's (that is, she loses her sanity). Her demons chase her to her bedroom, where she hysterically grabs a gun from her nightstand, and takes her own life.       Check out Lynch's clues--there's much more to them than what I've included here. He's a master--I don't think he produces a frame of film without agonizing over it for weeks, and I highly doubt someone who produced something as lovingly detailed as this film let any inconsistencies or gaffes slip past him. What a movie this is--I'll never forget it.

It's about time they got the credit they so desperately deserve. The hero here is bassist James Jamerson, who revolutionized the way the bass is played.Here, you understand why.<br />The second disc contains single mixes and a couple of outtakes, but more importantly it contains a live recording of Marvin and the Funk Brothers performing most of the album and a medley of earlier hits. The recording is raw, the performance was a bit sloppy (nerves and lack of rehersal time), but nonetheless is powerful and astonishing. B00006GO9Q|5.0|\\"In a Silent Way\\" belongs to the most influential and revolutionary albums of all time. Unlike many other jazz albums made in the fabulous sixties, it manages to be revolutionary in the most pleasant and accessible way, a quality only real geniuses can afford. This is a transitional album for Miles. In many ways it continues his works made since mid-sixties with the famous core Quintet (Davis, Hancock, Shorter, Carter, Williams), sometimes extended with guitars and other instruments, sometimes modified (Dave Holland replaced Ron Carter etc). When I think about Miles' long and varied career, I often think about these years as the most creative and intriguing, notwithstanding his great works with Wynton Kelly, Bill Evans and Gill Evans Orchestra. There is some exceptional, cosmic mood here that belongs to this time (compare Pink Floyd's \\"Piper Ate The Gates Of Dawn\\" or Moody Blues \\"In Search Of The Lost Chord\\" and their subsequent albums on the rock side). I think there was some radiating of the era, its social and moral radicalism, countless new trends emerging and flourishing. While experimenting often resulted in excess (free jazz, endless psychedelic jams, many recordings of Zappa or Beefheart) and produced recordings that were popular then but rarely listened to nowadays, these Miles' works are very concise and remain absolutely timeless. Moderately electrified, IASW, continues this path, but also anticipates the new era of Miles - the seventies with its long, improvised, noisy, distorted fusion jams. Many musicians joined here, who later took part in these jams - Corea, Zawinul, McLaughlin. While IASW marked the beginning of the new era, perfectly continued with Bitches Brew or Big Fun, further albums were the way downhill to almost unlistenable on Agharta or Pangaea (1974 and 75 appropriately), last before his 5-year retirement. While technically excellent, these jams seemed chaotic and completely soulless, and were for the seventies the same as long psychedelic dull jams were for the sixties.<BR>IASW consists also of two long jams, but they are exceptionally moody, fresh and high spirited. (Similar atmosphere was created before on \\"Circle In The Round\\" recorded in 1967, also a long, 27 minute-long track).<BR>They consist mostly of quiet, moody soundscapes created by trumpet, electric piano and guitar in forefront, backed by the hypnotizing rhythm section. There are also some funkier fragments in the second track.<BR>These recordings were so great that even Miles could not do it again, although he tried hard. There had to be something exceptional in late sixties (IASW was recorded in 1969) that never occurred again, resulting in so many timeless works of popular art. IASW stands out as one of the greatest, and belongs to the best works of Davis. Must own for any music fan, no matter if you are into rock, jazz, metal or techno. Indispensable!

B00008S2VG|5.0|Well I for one, although my two cents aren't necessarily worth much, positively ADORE this latest album by Madonna. I think the music, melodies, hooks, phrasing, beats, production, and her voice are just impeccable...it is everything I had hoped this album would be, and more. \\"Music\\" was a disappointment for me because, after the lush sound of Ray of Light, it seemed very disjointed and more a collection of singles than an album. However, fortunately on this album, Madonna and Mirwais stick it out throughout the album, and as a result, the album as a whole flows much better than its predecessor. Although Ray of Light has indeed stood the test of time and was, until the release of American Life, her best album of all time, it is my belief that \\"American Life\\" surpasses the genius that was Ray of Light. I think Madonna opens up much more with this album, and I think it is a deeper look into the psyche of Madonna than ROL. Hopefully I'll have the same sentiments in five years... <br />For me, the first three songs are really entertaining to listen to all together. Her vocals in the first two songs are much more the Madonna of old than what we've been hearing lately. Wonderful. I particularly like Hollywood (Sheryl Crow-esque) and I think, contrary to popular opinion apparently, that I'm so stupid is a great song. It's a sort of more in your face sounding track and it reminds me of the Madonna days of yore when she would never pull a video because she might offend people. But that's all in the past, and this is the present and future of Madonna's career. <br />I love her use of guitar and her naked voice on this album. Songs like Nothing Fails, Love Profusion, and X-static process are brilliant partly because of the honesty of Madonna's lyrics, but mostly because of the risks she is taking with her music. Madonna has always been about flash and image, but now she is sort of stripping down the barriers and finding herself, and that fact is revealed in the stripped down sound of the majority of this album. Her sound veers more towards Joni Mitchell than the Kylie sounding contemporary hits she generally releases. I love Mirwais's vocals on the beautiful Love Profusion, and Xstatic process is possibly the most touching ballad I've ever heard. I cried upon my first listen. Easy Ride is sheer genius, continuing the strings of Die another day into a beautiful ballad and ending from a sporadic mess of vocal and strings to a lovely melancholic final chord. Nothing fails in particular is a glorious peak for her career. If this song is not released it will be an even greater travesty than Impressive Instant. The choir in particular raises this song to a level unparalleled by any other Madonna single that I can think of. Superb. <br />I LOVE Mother and Father, the part with \\"I've got to give it up, find someone to love me\\" has been incessantly stuck in my head for days now, and overall is one of the more catchy songs she's done in the last decade. I think Die another day makes a lot more sense as a part of this album, and in context, it is a really slamming track. The strings and lyrics really fit the mood and add to the atmosphere Madonna has created. <br />I think that although ROL was an intensely personal album, but I believe that it was just the precursor to this one. This is the album Madonna was born to make. Every single song is brimming with emotion and Madonna baring her soul for the entire world to see. The fact that she manages to say what she's thinking in a way that keeps you humming the tunes over and over again in your head is just a small part of what makes her, and this album, so wonderful. I love every track on this album, and I think it's an encouraging sign that the fans opinions on what the next single should be are so diverse. That's always a good sign for an album. <br />But regardless of how this album and the singles from it perform on the charts, I absolutely adore it, and I am endlessly grateful to Madonna for going with her instincts and producing a semi \\"underground\\" (well relatively compared to everything else on the pop charts right now) album that isn't necessarily the best decision marketing-wise, but in the end will give her more credit as a musician, performer, singer, superstar, writer, producer, and most importantly, human who is realizing after a lifetime of trial and error, that nothing is what it seems. But at least we've been with her along the way, and maybe a select few in our current society will take her example, and follow their own paths rather than like sheep follow the beck and call of what is popular at the time but never leads to happiness. I dream of the day when American Life will again be about freedom of speech and religion and the freedom to be yourself (the very standards Madonna lives by), rather than repression of individuality for the safety of conformity.

An obviously inebriated Jimbo sounds like he couldn't decide whether to throw up or to go to sleep, so instead he went to the studio and cut some tracks. His vocals are shockingly [bad] and painfully off-key. He actually SOUNDS fat! Seriously...check it out!
